	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Latin America</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Latinoamérica" redirects here. For Latin Americans, see <a href="/wiki/Latin_Americans" title="Latin Americans">Latin Americans</a>. For the song, see <a href="/wiki/Latinoam%C3%A9rica_(song)" title="Latinoamérica (song)">Latinoamérica (song)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Region of the Americas where Romance languages are primarily spoken</div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Latin America<sup id="cite_ref-a_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-a-1">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" World</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Latin America</b><sup id="cite_ref-a_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-a-1">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> is a group of countries and dependencies in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a> where <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a> such as Spanish, Portuguese, and French are predominantly spoken; it is broader than the terms <a href="/wiki/Ibero-America" title="Ibero-America">Ibero-America</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_America" title="Hispanic America">Hispanic America</a>. The term "Latin America" was first used in an 1856 conference with the title "Initiative of the Americas. Idea for a Federal Congress of the Republics" (Iniciativa de la América. Idea de un Congreso Federal de las Repúblicas),<sup id="cite_ref-bilbao_f_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bilbao_f-8">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> by the Chilean politician <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Bilbao" title="Francisco Bilbao">Francisco Bilbao</a>. The term was used also by <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a>'s French government in the 1860s as <i>Amérique latine</i> to consider <a href="/wiki/Francophonie" title="Francophonie">French-speaking</a> territories in the Americas (<a href="/wiki/French_Canadians" title="French Canadians">French Canadians</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_Louisiana" title="French Louisiana">French Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guadeloupe" title="Guadeloupe">Guadeloupe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martinique" title="Martinique">Martinique</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collectivity_of_Saint_Martin" title="Collectivity of Saint Martin">Saint Martin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Barth%C3%A9lemy" title="Saint Barthélemy">Saint Barthélemy</a>), along with the larger group of countries where Spanish and Portuguese languages prevailed, including the Spanish-speaking portions of the United States (<a href="/wiki/Southwestern_United_States" title="Southwestern United States">Southwestern United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-Britton_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britton-9">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Today, areas of Canada (such as <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>) and the United States (with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-time.com_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time.com-10">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> where Spanish, Portuguese and French are predominant are typically not included in definitions of Latin America.
</p><p>Latin America consists of 13 dependencies and 20 countries which cover an area that stretches from the northern border of Mexico to the southern tip of South America, including the Caribbean. It has an area of approximately 19,197,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (7,412,000&#160;sq mi),<sup id="cite_ref-worldbank-ruralenv_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldbank-ruralenv-2">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> almost 13% of the Earth's land surface area. As of 2016, its population was estimated at more than  639 million<sup id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-3">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-d_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-d-4">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> and in 2014, Latin America had a combined <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">nominal GDP</a> of US$5,573,397 million<sup id="cite_ref-Imf.org_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Imf.org-11">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">GDP PPP</a> of 7,531,585 million USD.<sup id="cite_ref-Imf.org_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Imf.org-11">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-12">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_definitions">Etymology and definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mural_panoramico.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Mural_panoramico.JPG/440px-Mural_panoramico.JPG" decoding="async" width="440" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Mural_panoramico.JPG/660px-Mural_panoramico.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Mural_panoramico.JPG/880px-Mural_panoramico.JPG 2x" data-file-width="9336" data-file-height="2888" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mural_panoramico.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Presencia_de_Am%C3%A9rica_Latina" title="Presencia de América Latina">Presencia de América Latina</a> (<i>Presence of Latin America</i>, 1964–65) is a 300 square meters (3,200 sq ft) mural at the hall of the Arts House of the University of Concepción, Chile. It is also known as <i>Latin America's Integration</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>The idea that a part of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> has a linguistic affinity with the Romance cultures as a whole can be traced back to the 1830s, in the writing of the French <a href="/wiki/Saint-Simonianism" title="Saint-Simonianism">Saint-Simonian</a> <a href="/wiki/Michel_Chevalier" title="Michel Chevalier">Michel Chevalier</a>, who postulated that this part of the Americas was inhabited by people of a "<a href="/wiki/Italic_peoples" title="Italic peoples">Latin race</a>", and that it could, therefore, ally itself with "<a href="/wiki/Romance-speaking_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Romance-speaking Europe">Latin Europe</a>", ultimately overlapping the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a>, in a struggle with "<a href="/wiki/Germanic-speaking_Europe" title="Germanic-speaking Europe">Teutonic Europe</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Anglo-America" title="Anglo-America">Anglo-Saxon America</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Slavic_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic Europe">Slavic Europe</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-mignolo_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mignolo-13">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>  Further investigations of the concept of Latin America are by Michel Gobat in the <i><a href="/wiki/American_Historical_Review" class="mw-redirect" title="American Historical Review">American Historical Review</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Michel_Gobat_2013_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michel_Gobat_2013-14">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> the studies of Leslie Bethell,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and the monograph by Mauricio Tenorio-Trillo, <i>Latin America: The Allure and Power of an Idea</i> (2017).<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian John Leddy Phelan located the origins of “Latin America” in the French occupation of Mexico. His argument is that French imperialists used the concept of "Latin" America as a way to counter British imperialism, as well as to challenge the German threat to France.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>  The idea of a "Latin race" was then taken up by Latin American intellectuals and political leaders of the mid- and late-nineteenth century, who no longer looked to Spain or Portugal as cultural models, but rather to France.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>  French ruler Napoleon III had a strong interest in extending French commercial and political power in the region he and his business promoter Felix Belly called “Latin America” to emphasize the shared Latin background of France with the former colonies of Spain and Portugal. This led to Napoleon's failed attempt to take military control of Mexico in the 1860s.<sup id="cite_ref-Britton_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britton-9">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, though Phelan thesis is still frequently mentioned in the U.S. academy, two Latin American historians, the Uruguayan <a href="/wiki/Arturo_Ardao" title="Arturo Ardao">Arturo Ardao</a> and the Chilean <a href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miguel_Rojas_Mix" class="extiw" title="es:Miguel Rojas Mix">Miguel Rojas Mix</a> proved decades ago that the term "Latin America" was used earlier than Phelan claimed, and the first use of the term was completely opposite to support imperialist projects in the Americas. Ardao wrote about this subject in his book <i>Génesis de la idea y el nombre de América latina</i> (Genesis of the Idea and the Name of Latin America, 1980),<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and Miguel Rojas Mix in his article "Bilbao y el hallazgo de América latina: Unión continental, socialista y libertaria" (Bilbao and the Finding of Latin America: a Continental, Socialist and Libertarian Union, 1986).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> As Michel Gobat reminds in his article "The Invention of Latin America: A Transnational History of Anti-Imperialism, Democracy, and Race", "Arturo Ardao, Miguel Rojas Mix, and Aims McGuinness have revealed [that] the term 'Latin America' had already been used in 1856 by Central and South Americans protesting U.S. expansion into the Southern Hemisphere".<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Edward Shawcross summarizes Ardao's and Rojas Mix's findings in the following way: "Ardao identified the term in a poem by a Colombian diplomat and intellectual resident in France, José María Torres Caicedo, published on 15 February 1857 in a French based Spanish-language newspaper, while Rojas Mix located it in a speech delivered in France by the radical liberal Chilean politician Francisco Bilbao in June 1856".<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the late 1850s, the term was being used in local California newspapers such as <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=El_Clamor_Publico&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="El Clamor Publico (page does not exist)">El Clamor Publico</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Californios" class="mw-redirect" title="Californios">californios</a> writing about <i>America latina</i> and <i>latinoamerica</i>, and identifying as <i><a href="/wiki/Latinos" class="mw-redirect" title="Latinos">latinos</a></i> as the abbreviated term for their "hemispheric membership in <i>la raza latina</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-Gutierrez-2016_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gutierrez-2016-23">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>So, regarding when the words "Latin" and "America" were combined for the first time in a printed work, the term "Latin America" was first used in 1856 in a conference by the Chilean politician <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Bilbao" title="Francisco Bilbao">Francisco Bilbao</a> in Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The conference had the title "Initiative of the America. Idea for a Federal Congress of Republics."<sup id="cite_ref-bilbao_f_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bilbao_f-8">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The following year the Colombian writer <a href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Torres_Caicedo" class="extiw" title="es:José María Torres Caicedo">José María Torres Caicedo</a> also used the term in his poem "The Two Americas".<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Two events related with the U.S. played a central role in both works. The first event happened less than a decade before the publication of Bilbao's and Torres Caicedo works: the <a href="/wiki/Mexican-_American_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexican- American war">Mexican–American War</a>, after which Mexico lost a third of its territory. The second event, the <a href="/wiki/Walker_affair" title="Walker affair">Walker affair</a>, happened the same year both works were written: the decision by U.S. president <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a> to recognize the regime recently established in Nicaragua by American <a href="/wiki/William_Walker_(filibuster)" title="William Walker (filibuster)">William Walker</a> and his band of filibusters who ruled Nicaragua for nearly a year (1856–57) and attempted to reinstate slavery there, where it had been already abolished for three decades
</p><p>In both Bilbao's and Torres Caicedo's works, the <a href="/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American_War" title="Mexican–American War">Mexican-American War</a> and Walker's expedition to Nicaragua are explicitly mentioned as examples of dangers for the region.  For Bilbao, "Latin America" was not a geographical concept, since he excluded Brazil, Paraguay and Mexico.  Both authors also ask for the union of all Latin American countries as the only way to defend their territories against further foreign U.S. interventions. Both rejected also European imperialism, claiming that the return of European countries to non-democratic forms of government was another danger for Latin American countries, and used the same word to describe the state of European politics at the time: "despotism."  Several years later, during the <a href="/wiki/French_invasion_of_Mexico" class="mw-redirect" title="French invasion of Mexico">French invasion of Mexico</a>, Bilbao wrote another work, "Emancipation of the Spirit in America," where he asked all Latin American countries to support the Mexican cause against France, and rejected French imperialism in Asia, Africa, Europe and the Americas. He asked Latin American intellectuals to search for their "intellectual emancipation" by abandoning all French ideas, claiming that France was: "Hypocrite, because she [France] calls herself protector of the Latin race just to subject it to her exploitation regime; treacherous, because she speaks of freedom and nationality, when, unable to conquer freedom for herself, she enslaves others instead!"<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, as Michel Gobat puts it, the term Latin America itself had an "anti-imperial genesis," and their creators were far from supporting any form of imperialism in the region, or in any other place of the globe.
</p><p>However, in France the term Latin America was used with the opposite intention. It was employed by the French Empire of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a> during the <a href="/wiki/French_invasion_of_Mexico" class="mw-redirect" title="French invasion of Mexico">French invasion of Mexico</a> as a way to include France among countries with influence in the Americas and to exclude <a href="/wiki/Anglo_America" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo America">Anglophone countries</a>. It played a role in his campaign to imply cultural kinship of the region with France, transform France into a cultural and political leader of the area, and install <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I_of_Mexico" title="Maximilian I of Mexico">Maximilian of Habsburg</a> as emperor of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Mexican_Empire" title="Second Mexican Empire">Second Mexican Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> This term was also used in 1861 by French scholars in <i>La revue des races Latines,</i> a magazine dedicated to the <a href="/wiki/Pan-Latinism" title="Pan-Latinism">Pan-Latinism</a> movement.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_definitions">Contemporary definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Contemporary definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Latin_America_regions.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Latin_America_regions.svg/220px-Latin_America_regions.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Latin_America_regions.svg/330px-Latin_America_regions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Latin_America_regions.svg/440px-Latin_America_regions.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Latin_America_regions.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 4 common subregions in Latin America</div></div></div>
<ul><li><i>Latin America</i> generally refers to territories in the Americas where the Spanish, Portuguese or French languages prevail: Mexico, most of Central and South America, and in the Caribbean, Cuba, the Dominican Republic, Haiti, Puerto Rico, and Quebec.  <i>Latin America</i> is, therefore, defined as all those parts of the Americas that were once part of the Spanish, Portuguese and French Empires.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> By this definition, Latin America is coterminous with <a href="/wiki/Ibero-America" title="Ibero-America">Ibero-America</a> when excluding the minority French-speaking territories ("Iberian America").<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The term is sometimes used more broadly to refer to all of the Americas south of the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-Torres_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Torres-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> thus including <a href="/wiki/The_Guianas" title="The Guianas">the Guianas</a> (<a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Caribbean" title="Commonwealth Caribbean">Anglophone Caribbean</a> (and <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a>); the <a href="/wiki/French_West_Indies" title="French West Indies">Francophone Caribbean</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Caribbean" title="Dutch Caribbean">Dutch Caribbean</a>. This definition emphasizes a similar <a href="/wiki/Socioeconomics" title="Socioeconomics">socioeconomic</a> history of the region, which was characterized by formal or <a href="/wiki/Neocolonialism" title="Neocolonialism">informal colonialism</a>, rather than cultural aspects (see, for example, <a href="/wiki/Dependency_theory" title="Dependency theory">dependency theory</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> As such, some sources avoid this oversimplification by using the phrase "Latin America and the Caribbean" instead, as in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_geoscheme_for_the_Americas" title="United Nations geoscheme for the Americas">United Nations geoscheme for the Americas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In a more literal definition, which is close to the semantic origin, <i>Latin America</i> designates countries in the Americas where a <a href="/wiki/Romance_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Romance language">Romance language</a> (a language derived from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>) predominates: Spanish, Portuguese, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">creole languages</a> based upon these.<sup id="cite_ref-Torres_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Torres-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The distinction between <i>Latin America</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Anglo-America" title="Anglo-America">Anglo-America</a></i> is a convention based on the predominant languages in the Americas by which Romance-language and English-speaking cultures are distinguished. Neither area is culturally or linguistically homogeneous; in substantial portions of Latin America (e.g., highland <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>), <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native American</a> cultures and, to a lesser extent, Amerindian languages, are predominant, and in other areas, the influence of African cultures is strong (e.g., the Caribbean basin&#160;&#8211;&#32;including parts of <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>).
</p><p>The term is not without controversy.  Historian Mauricio Tenorio-Trillo explores at length the "allure and power" of the idea of Latin America. He remarks at the outset, "The idea of 'Latin America' ought to have vanished with the obsolescence of racial theory... But it is not easy to declare something dead when it can hardly be said to have existed," going on to say, "The term is here to stay, and it is important."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Following in the tradition of Chilean writer Francisco Bilbao, who excluded Brazil, Argentina and Paraguay from his early conceptualization of Latin America,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Chilean historian <a href="/wiki/Jaime_Eyzaguirre" title="Jaime Eyzaguirre">Jaime Eyzaguirre</a> has criticized the term Latin America for "disguising" and "diluting" the Spanish character of a region (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_America" title="Hispanic America">Hispanic America</a>) with the inclusion of nations that according to him do not share the same pattern of <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">conquest and colonization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subregions_and_countries">Subregions and countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Subregions and countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Latin America can be subdivided into several subregions based on geography, politics, demographics and culture. If defined as all of the Americas south of the United States, the basic geographical subregions are <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-GeoSEP_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GeoSEP-39">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> the latter contains further politico-geographical subdivisions such as the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Cone" title="Southern Cone">Southern Cone</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Guianas" title="The Guianas">the Guianas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Andean_states" title="Andean states">Andean states</a>. It may be subdivided on linguistic grounds into <a href="/wiki/Hispanic_America" title="Hispanic America">Hispanic America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_America" class="mw-redirect" title="Portuguese America">Portuguese America</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_America" title="French America">French America</a>.
</p>
