<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I_and_the_Zimmermann_Telegram">World War I and the Zimmermann Telegram</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: World War I and the Zimmermann Telegram">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zimmermann_Telegram.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Zimmermann_Telegram.jpeg/220px-Zimmermann_Telegram.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="294" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Zimmermann_Telegram.jpeg/330px-Zimmermann_Telegram.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Zimmermann_Telegram.jpeg/440px-Zimmermann_Telegram.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="708" data-file-height="945" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zimmermann_Telegram.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Zimmermann Telegram as it was sent from <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington</a> to Ambassador <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_von_Eckardt" title="Heinrich von Eckardt">Heinrich von Eckardt</a> (German ambassador to <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Zimmermann_Telegram" title="Zimmermann Telegram">Zimmermann Telegram</a> was a 1917 diplomatic proposal from the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> for <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mexico" title="History of Mexico">Mexico</a> to join an alliance with Germany in the event of the United States entering <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> against Germany. The proposal was intercepted and decoded by British intelligence. The revelation of the contents outraged the American public and swayed public opinion. President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> moved to arm American merchant ships in order to defend themselves against German submarines, which had started to attack them. The news helped generate support for the <a href="/wiki/American_entry_into_World_War_I" title="American entry into World War I">United States declaration of war on Germany</a> in April of that year.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The message came as a <a href="/wiki/Code_(cryptography)" title="Code (cryptography)">coded</a> telegram dispatched by the Foreign Secretary of the German Empire, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Zimmermann" title="Arthur Zimmermann">Arthur Zimmermann</a>, on January 16, 1917. The message was sent to the German ambassador of Mexico, <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_von_Eckardt" title="Heinrich von Eckardt">Heinrich von Eckardt</a>. Zimmermann sent the telegram in anticipation of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_U-boat_campaign_of_World_War_I#1917:_Renewed_&quot;unrestricted&quot;_campaign" title="Atlantic U-boat campaign of World War I">resumption of unrestricted submarine warfare</a> by Germany on 1 February, an act which Germany presumed would lead to war. The telegram instructed Ambassador Eckardt that if the U.S. appeared certain to enter the war, he was to approach the Mexican Government with a proposal for a military alliance, with funding from Germany. As part of the alliance, Germany would assist Mexico in reconquering Texas and the Southwest. Eckardt was instructed to urge Mexico to help broker an alliance between Germany and Japan. Mexico, in the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">Mexican Revolution</a>, far weaker militarily, economically and politically than the U.S., ignored the proposal; after the U.S. entered the war, it officially rejected it.
</p>
<h4><span id="Brazil.27s_participation_in_World_War_II"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Brazil's_participation_in_World_War_II">Brazil's participation in World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Brazil&#039;s participation in World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After World War I, in which Brazil was an ally of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, the country realized it needed a more capable army but did not have the technology to create it. In 1919, the French Military Mission was established by the French Commission in Brazil. Their main goal was to contain the inner rebellions in Brazil. They tried to assist the army by bringing them up to the European military standard but constant civil missions did not prepare them for <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.
</p><p>Brazil's President, <a href="/wiki/Get%C3%BAlio_Vargas" title="Getúlio Vargas">Getúlio Vargas</a>, wanted to industrialize Brazil, allowing it to be more competitive with other countries. He reached out to Germany, Italy, France, and the United States to act as trade allies. Many Italian and German people immigrated to Brazil many years before World War II began thus creating a Nazi influence. The immigrants held high positions in government and the armed forces. It was recently found that 9,000 war criminals escaped to South America, including Croats, Ukrainians, Russians and other western Europeans who aided the Nazi war machine. Most, perhaps as many as 5,000, went to Argentina; between 1,500 and 2,000 are thought to have made it to Brazil; around 500 to 1,000 to Chile; and the rest to Paraguay and Uruguay.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> It was not a secret that Vargas had an admiration for Hitler's Nazi Germany and its Führer. He even let German Luftwaffe build secret air forces around Brazil.
This alliance with Germany became Brazil's second best trade alliance behind the United States.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Massarosaw.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Massarosaw.jpg/220px-Massarosaw.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Massarosaw.jpg/330px-Massarosaw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Massarosaw.jpg/440px-Massarosaw.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1261" data-file-height="915" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Massarosaw.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Brazilian soldiers greet Italian civilians in the city of <a href="/wiki/Massarosa" title="Massarosa">Massarosa</a>, September 1944. Brazil was the only independent Latin American country to send ground troops to fight in WWII.</div></div></div>
<p>Brazil continued to try to remain neutral to the United States and Germany because it was trying to make sure it could continue to be a place of interest for both opposing countries. Brazil attended continental meetings in Buenos Aires, Argentina (1936); Lima, Peru (1938); and Havana, Cuba (1940) that obligated them to agree to defend any part of the Americas if they were to be attacked. Eventually, Brazil decided to stop trading with Germany once Germany started attacking offshore trading ships resulting in Germany declaring a blockade against the Americas in the Atlantic Ocean. Furthermore, Germany also ensured that they would be attacking the Americas soon.
</p><p>Once the German submarines attacked unarmed Brazilian trading ships, President Vargas met with the United States President Franklin D. Roosevelt to discuss how they could retaliate. On January 22, 1942, Brazil officially ended all relations with Germany, Japan, and Italy, becoming a part of the Allies.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Expeditionary_Force" title="Brazilian Expeditionary Force">Brazilian Expeditionary Force</a> was sent to Naples, Italy to fight for democracy. Brazil was the only Latin American country to send troops to Europe. Initially, Brazil wanted to only provide resources and shelter for the war to have a chance of gaining a high postwar status but ended up sending 25,000 men to fight.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After World War II, the United States and Latin America continued to have a close relationship. For example, USAID created family planning programs in Latin America combining the NGOs already in place, providing the women in largely Catholic areas access to contraception.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico_and_World_War_II">Mexico and World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Mexico and World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mexico" title="History of Mexico">History of Mexico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin_America_during_World_War_II" title="Latin America during World War II">Latin America during World War II</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BraceroProgram.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/BraceroProgram.jpg/220px-BraceroProgram.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/BraceroProgram.jpg/330px-BraceroProgram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/ba/BraceroProgram.jpg 2x" data-file-width="421" data-file-height="429" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BraceroProgram.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first Braceros arrive in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>, 1942.</div></div></div>
<p>Mexico entered World War II in response to German attacks on Mexican ships.  The <i><a href="/wiki/SS_Potrero_del_Llano" title="SS Potrero del Llano">Potrero del Llano</a>,</i> originally an Italian tanker, had been seized in port by the <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexican</a> government in April 1941 and renamed in honor of a region in Veracruz. It was attacked and crippled by the <a href="/wiki/German_submarine_U-564" title="German submarine U-564">German submarine&#160;<i>U-564</i></a> on 13 May 1942. The attack killed 13 of 35 crewmen.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> On 20 May 1942, a second tanker, <i><a href="/wiki/Faja_de_Oro" class="mw-redirect" title="Faja de Oro">Faja de Oro</a>,</i> also a seized Italian ship, was attacked and sunk by the <a href="/wiki/German_submarine_U-160_(1941)" title="German submarine U-160 (1941)">German submarine&#160;<i>U-160</i></a>, killing 10 of 37 crewmen. In response, President <a href="/wiki/Manuel_%C3%81vila_Camacho" title="Manuel Ávila Camacho">Manuel Ávila Camacho</a> and the Mexican government declared war on the Axis powers on 22 May 1942.
</p><p>A large part of Mexico's contribution to the war came through an agreement January 1942 that allowed Mexican nationals living in the United States to join the American armed forces. As many as 250,000 Mexicans served in this way.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In the final year of the war, Mexico sent one air squadron to serve under the Mexican flag: the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Air_Force" title="Mexican Air Force">Mexican Air Force</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Escuadr%C3%B3n_201" class="mw-redirect" title="Escuadrón 201">Escuadrón Aéreo de Pelea 201</a></i> (201st Fighter Squadron), which saw combat in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> in the war against <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Japan">Imperial Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Mexico was the only Latin-American country to send troops to the Asia-Pacific theatre of the war. In addition to those in the armed forces, tens of thousands of Mexican men were hired as farm workers in the United States during the war years through the <a href="/wiki/Bracero_program" title="Bracero program"><i>Bracero</i> program</a>, which continued and expanded in the decades after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>World War II helped spark an era of rapid industrialization known as the <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Miracle" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexican Miracle">Mexican Miracle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Mexico supplied the United States with more strategic raw materials than any other country, and American aid spurred the growth of industry.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> President Ávila was able to use the increased revenue to improve the country's credit, invest in infrastructure, subsidize food, and raise wages.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Involvement_in_World_War_II">Involvement in World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Involvement in World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There was a Nazi influence in certain parts of the region, but Jewish migration from Europe during the war continued. Only a few people recognized or knew about the Holocaust.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, numerous military bases were built during the war by the United States, but some also by the Germans. Even now, unexploded bombs from the second world war that need to be made safe still remain.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Cold_War_.281945.E2.80.931992.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_War_(1945–1992)">Cold War (1945–1992) <span id="Cold_War"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Cold War (1945–1992)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Operation_Condor" title="Operation Condor">Operation Condor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_American_States" title="Organization of American States">Organization of American States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inter-American_Treaty_of_Reciprocal_Assistance" title="Inter-American Treaty of Reciprocal Assistance">Inter-American Treaty of Reciprocal Assistance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_Progress" title="Alliance for Progress">Alliance for Progress</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NASAburningbrazil.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/NASAburningbrazil.jpg/220px-NASAburningbrazil.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/NASAburningbrazil.jpg/330px-NASAburningbrazil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/NASAburningbrazil.jpg/440px-NASAburningbrazil.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="433" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NASAburningbrazil.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burning forest in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>. The removal of forest to make way for <a href="/wiki/Cattle_ranching" class="mw-redirect" title="Cattle ranching">cattle ranching</a> was the leading cause of deforestation in the Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Amazon_rainforest" title="Amazon rainforest">Amazon rainforest</a> from the mid-1960s. <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">Soybeans</a> have become one of the most important contributors to deforestation in the Brazilian Amazon.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The Great Depression caused Latin America to grow at a slow rate, separating it from leading industrial democracies. The two world wars and U.S. Depression also made Latin American countries favor internal economic development, leading Latin America to adopt the policy of <a href="/wiki/Import_substitution_industrialization" title="Import substitution industrialization">import substitution industrialization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-History_of_Latin_America_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_Latin_America-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Countries also renewed emphasis on exports. Brazil began selling automobiles to other countries, and some Latin American countries set up plants to assemble imported parts, letting other countries take advantage of Latin America's low labor costs. Colombia began to export flowers, emeralds and coffee grains and gold, becoming the world's second-leading flower exporter.
</p><p>Economic integration was called for, to attain economies that could compete with the economies of the United States or Europe. Starting in the 1960s with the Latin American Free Trade Association and Central American Common Market, Latin American countries worked toward economic integration.
</p><p>In efforts to help regain global economic strength, the U.S. began to heavily assist countries involved in World War II at the expense of Latin America. Markets that were previously unopposed as a result of the war in Latin America grew stagnant as the rest of the world no longer needed their goods.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Reforms">Reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Large countries like Argentina called for reforms to lessen the disparity of wealth between the rich and the poor, which has been a long problem in Latin America that stunted economic growth.<sup id="cite_ref-Kaufman_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaufman-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Advances in public health caused an explosion of population growth, making it difficult to provide social services. Education expanded, and social security systems introduced, but benefits usually went to the middle class, not the poor. As a result, the disparity of wealth increased. Increasing inflation and other factors caused countries to be unwilling to fund social development programs to help the poor.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bureaucratic_authoritarianism">Bureaucratic authoritarianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Bureaucratic authoritarianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Bureaucratic authoritarianism was practised in Brazil after 1964, in Argentina, and in Chile under Augusto Pinochet, in a response to harsh economic conditions. It rested on the conviction that no democracy could take the harsh measures to curb inflation, reassure investors, and quicken economic growth quickly and effectively. Though inflation fell sharply, industrial production dropped with the decline of official protection.<sup id="cite_ref-Kaufman_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kaufman-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="US_relations">US relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: US relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Latin_America%E2%80%93United_States_relations" title="Latin America–United States relations">Latin America–United States relations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foreign_interventions_by_the_United_States" title="Foreign interventions by the United States">Foreign interventions by the United States</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_involvement_in_regime_change_in_Latin_America" title="United States involvement in regime change in Latin America">United States involvement in regime change in Latin America</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reuni%C3%B3n_Pinochet_-_Kissinger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Reuni%C3%B3n_Pinochet_-_Kissinger.jpg/220px-Reuni%C3%B3n_Pinochet_-_Kissinger.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Reuni%C3%B3n_Pinochet_-_Kissinger.jpg/330px-Reuni%C3%B3n_Pinochet_-_Kissinger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Reuni%C3%B3n_Pinochet_-_Kissinger.jpg/440px-Reuni%C3%B3n_Pinochet_-_Kissinger.jpg 2x" data-file-width="680" data-file-height="468" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reuni%C3%B3n_Pinochet_-_Kissinger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chilean dictator <a href="/wiki/Augusto_Pinochet" title="Augusto Pinochet">Augusto Pinochet</a> shaking hands with U.S. Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> in 1976</div></div></div>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and the beginning of a <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> between the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, US diplomats became interested in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, and Latin America, and frequently<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (June 2014)">vague</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> waged proxy wars against the Soviet Union in these countries. The US sought to stop the spread of communism. Latin American countries generally sided with the US in the Cold War period, even though they were neglected since the US's concern with communism were focused in Europe and Asia, not Latin America. Between 1946 and 1959 Latin America received only 2% of the United States foreign aid despite having poor conditions similar to the main recipients of The <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Some Latin American governments also complained of the US support in the overthrow of some nationalist governments, and intervention through the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a>. In 1947, the US Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Act_of_1947" title="National Security Act of 1947">National Security Act</a>, which created the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Security_Council" title="United States National Security Council">National Security Council</a> in response to the United States's growing obsession with anti-communism.<sup id="cite_ref-Dominguez_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dominguez-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1954, when <a href="/wiki/Jacobo_Arbenz" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacobo Arbenz">Jacobo Arbenz</a> of Guatemala accepted the support of communists and attacked holdings of the <a href="/wiki/United_Fruit_Company" title="United Fruit Company">United Fruit Company</a>, the US decided to assist Guatemalan counter-revolutionaries in overthrowing Arbenz.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> These interventionist tactics featured the use of the CIA rather than the military, which was used in Latin America for the majority of the Cold War in events including the overthrow of <a href="/wiki/Salvador_Allende" title="Salvador Allende">Salvador Allende</a>. Latin America was more concerned with issues of economic development, while the United States focused on fighting communism, even though the presence of communism was small in Latin America.<sup id="cite_ref-Dominguez_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dominguez-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuban_Revolution">Cuban Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Cuban Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By 1959, Cuba was afflicted with a corrupt dictatorship under Batista, and <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a> ousted Batista that year and set up the first communist state in the hemisphere. The United States imposed a trade embargo on Cuba, and combined with Castro's expropriation of private enterprises, this was detrimental to the Cuban economy.<sup id="cite_ref-History_of_Latin_America_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_Latin_America-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Around Latin America, rural guerrilla conflict and urban terrorism increased, inspired by the Cuban example. The United States put down these rebellions by supporting Latin American countries in their counter-guerrilla operations through the Alliance for Progress launched by President John F. Kennedy. This thrust appeared to be successful. A Marxist, Salvador Allende, became president of Chile in 1970, but was overthrown three years later in a military coup backed by the United States. Despite civil war, high crime and political instability, most Latin American countries eventually adopted bourgeois liberal democracies while Cuba maintained its socialist system.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion">Bay of Pigs Invasion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Bay of Pigs Invasion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion" title="Bay of Pigs Invasion">Bay of Pigs Invasion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CheyFidel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/CheyFidel.jpg/170px-CheyFidel.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/CheyFidel.jpg/255px-CheyFidel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/CheyFidel.jpg/340px-CheyFidel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1197" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CheyFidel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Che_Guevara" title="Che Guevara">Che Guevara</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Castro</a>, photographed by <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Korda" title="Alberto Korda">Alberto Korda</a> in 1961</div></div></div>
<p>Encouraged by the success of Guatemala in the <a href="/wiki/1954_Guatemalan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1954 Guatemalan coup d&#39;état">1954 Guatemalan coup d'état</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> in 1960, the U.S. decided to support an attack on Cuba by anti-Castro rebels. The Bay of Pigs invasion was an unsuccessful invasion of Cuba in 1961, financed by the U.S. through the CIA, to overthrow <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>. The incident proved to be very embarrassing for the new Kennedy administration.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alliance_for_Progress">Alliance for Progress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Alliance for Progress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>President John F. Kennedy initiated the <a href="/wiki/Alliance_for_Progress" title="Alliance for Progress">Alliance for Progress</a> in 1961, to establish economic cooperation between the U.S. and Latin America. The Alliance would provide $20 billion for reform in Latin America, and counterinsurgency measures. Instead, the reform failed because of the simplistic theory that guided it and the lack of experienced American experts who could understand Latin American customs.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nicaraguan_Revolution">Nicaraguan Revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Nicaraguan Revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Following the American occupation of Nicaragua in 1912, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Banana_Wars" title="Banana Wars">Banana Wars</a>, the Somoza family political dynasty came to power, and would rule Nicaragua until their ouster in 1979 during the <a href="/wiki/Nicaraguan_Revolution" title="Nicaraguan Revolution">Nicaraguan Revolution</a>. The era of Somoza family rule was characterized by strong U.S. support for the government and its military as well as a heavy reliance on U.S.-based multi-national corporations. The Nicaraguan Revolution (Spanish: Revolución Nicaragüense or Revolución Popular Sandinista) encompassed the rising opposition to the Somoza dictatorship in the 1960s and 1970s, the campaign led by the Sandinista National Liberation Front (FSLN) to violently oust the dictatorship in 1978–79, the subsequent efforts of the FSLN to govern Nicaragua from 1979 until 1990 and the <a href="/wiki/Nicaraguan_Revolution#Contra_War" title="Nicaraguan Revolution">Contra War</a> which was waged between the FSLN and the <a href="/wiki/Contras" title="Contras">Contras</a> from 1981–1990.
</p><p>The Revolution marked a significant period in Nicaraguan history and revealed the country as one of the major proxy war battlegrounds of the Cold War with the events in the country rising to international attention. Although the initial overthrow of the Somoza regime in 1978–79 was a bloody affair, the Contra War of the 1980s took the lives of tens of thousands of Nicaraguans and was the subject of fierce international debate.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> During the 1980s both the FSLN (a Leftist collection of political parties) and the Contras (a rightist collection of counter-revolutionary groups) received large amounts of aid from the Cold War super-powers (respectively, the Soviet Union and the United States).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Washington_Consensus">Washington Consensus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Washington Consensus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Washington_Consensus" title="Washington Consensus">Washington Consensus</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Free_Trade_Area_of_the_Americas" title="Free Trade Area of the Americas">Free Trade Area of the Americas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ship_passing_through_Panama_Canal_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Ship_passing_through_Panama_Canal_01.jpg/220px-Ship_passing_through_Panama_Canal_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Ship_passing_through_Panama_Canal_01.jpg/330px-Ship_passing_through_Panama_Canal_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Ship_passing_through_Panama_Canal_01.jpg/440px-Ship_passing_through_Panama_Canal_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ship_passing_through_Panama_Canal_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Roll-on/roll-off" title="Roll-on/roll-off">Roll-on/roll-off</a> ships, such as this one pictured here at <a href="/wiki/Miraflores_(Panama)" title="Miraflores (Panama)">Miraflores locks</a>, are among the largest ships to pass through the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>. The canal cuts across the Isthmus of <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a> and is a key conduit for international maritime trade.</div></div></div>
<p>The set of specific economic policy prescriptions that were considered the "standard" reform package were promoted for crisis-wracked developing countries by Washington, D.C.-based institutions such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> (IMF), <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Department of the Treasury">US Department of the Treasury</a> during the 1980s and 1990s.
</p><p>In recent years, several Latin American countries led by socialist or other left wing governments&#160;&#8211;&#32;including Argentina and Venezuela&#160;&#8211;&#32;have campaigned for (and to some degree adopted) policies contrary to the Washington Consensus set of policies. (Other Latin countries with governments of the left, including Brazil, Mexico, Chile and Peru, have in practice adopted the bulk of the policies.) Also critical of the policies as actually promoted by the International Monetary Fund have been some US economists, such as <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stiglitz" title="Joseph Stiglitz">Joseph Stiglitz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dani_Rodrik" title="Dani Rodrik">Dani Rodrik</a>, who have challenged what are sometimes described as the "fundamentalist" policies of the International Monetary Fund and the US Treasury for what Stiglitz calls a "one size fits all" treatment of individual economies.
</p><p>The term has become associated with neoliberal policies in general and drawn into the broader debate over the expanding role of the free market, constraints upon the state, and US influence on other countries' national sovereignty.
</p><p>This politico-economical initiative was institutionalized in North America by 1994 <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">NAFTA</a>, and elsewhere in the Americas through a series of <a href="/wiki/Free_Trade_Area_of_the_Americas#Agreements" title="Free Trade Area of the Americas">like agreements</a>. The comprehensive <a href="/wiki/Free_Trade_Area_of_the_Americas" title="Free Trade Area of the Americas">Free Trade Area of the Americas</a> project, however, was rejected by most <a href="/wiki/Mercosur" title="Mercosur">South American countries</a> at the 2005 <a href="/wiki/4th_Summit_of_the_Americas" title="4th Summit of the Americas">4th Summit of the Americas</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turn_to_the_left">Turn to the left</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Turn to the left">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pink_tide" title="Pink tide">Pink tide</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UNASUR-15SEP2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/UNASUR-15SEP2008.jpg/220px-UNASUR-15SEP2008.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/UNASUR-15SEP2008.jpg/330px-UNASUR-15SEP2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/UNASUR-15SEP2008.jpg/440px-UNASUR-15SEP2008.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1397" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UNASUR-15SEP2008.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/UNASUR" class="mw-redirect" title="UNASUR">UNASUR</a> summit in the Palacio de la Moneda, Santiago de Chile</div></div></div>
<p>In most countries, since the 2000s left-wing political parties have risen to power.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The presidencies of <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez" title="Hugo Chávez">Hugo Chávez</a> in Venezuela, <a href="/wiki/Ricardo_Lagos" title="Ricardo Lagos">Ricardo Lagos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Bachelet" title="Michelle Bachelet">Michelle Bachelet</a> in Chile, <a href="/wiki/Lula_da_Silva" class="mw-redirect" title="Lula da Silva">Lula da Silva</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dilma_Rousseff" title="Dilma Rousseff">Dilma Rousseff</a> in Brazil, <a href="/wiki/N%C3%A9stor_Kirchner" title="Néstor Kirchner">Néstor Kirchner</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Cristina_Fern%C3%A1ndez_de_Kirchner" title="Cristina Fernández de Kirchner">Cristina Fernández</a> in Argentina, <a href="/wiki/Tabar%C3%A9_V%C3%A1zquez" title="Tabaré Vázquez">Tabaré Vázquez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mujica" title="José Mujica">José Mujica</a> in Uruguay, <a href="/wiki/Evo_Morales" title="Evo Morales">Evo Morales</a> in Bolivia, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Ortega" title="Daniel Ortega">Daniel Ortega</a> in Nicaragua, <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Correa" title="Rafael Correa">Rafael Correa</a> in Ecuador, <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Lugo" title="Fernando Lugo">Fernando Lugo</a> in Paraguay, <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Zelaya" title="Manuel Zelaya">Manuel Zelaya</a> in <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a> (removed from power by a <a href="/wiki/2009_Honduran_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2009 Honduran coup d&#39;état">coup d'état</a>), <a href="/wiki/Mauricio_Funes" title="Mauricio Funes">Mauricio Funes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salvador_S%C3%A1nchez_Cer%C3%A9n" title="Salvador Sánchez Cerén">Salvador Sánchez Cerén</a> in El Salvador are all part of this wave of <a href="/wiki/Left-wing" class="mw-redirect" title="Left-wing">left-wing</a> politicians who often declare themselves <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_American_integration" title="Latin American integration">Latin Americanists</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Anti-Imperialism" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Imperialism">anti-imperialists</a> (often implying <a href="/wiki/Anti-Americanism#Latin_America" title="Anti-Americanism">opposition to US</a> <a href="/wiki/Washington_consensus" class="mw-redirect" title="Washington consensus">policies towards the region</a>). A development of this has been the creation of the eight-member ALBA alliance, or "<a href="/wiki/Bolivarian_Alliance_for_the_Americas" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolivarian Alliance for the Americas">The Bolivarian Alliance for the Peoples of Our America</a>" (Spanish: <i>Alianza Bolivariana para los Pueblos de Nuestra América</i>) by some of the countries already mentioned. By June 2014, Honduras (<a href="/wiki/Juan_Orlando_Hern%C3%A1ndez" title="Juan Orlando Hernández">Juan Orlando Hernández</a>), Guatemala (<a href="/wiki/Otto_P%C3%A9rez_Molina" title="Otto Pérez Molina">Otto Pérez Molina</a>), and Panama (<a href="/wiki/Ricardo_Martinelli" title="Ricardo Martinelli">Ricardo Martinelli</a>) had right-wing governments.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_of_social_movements">Return of social movements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Return of social movements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:45._Humauaca_(1).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/45._Humauaca_%281%29.JPG/220px-45._Humauaca_%281%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/45._Humauaca_%281%29.JPG/330px-45._Humauaca_%281%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/45._Humauaca_%281%29.JPG/440px-45._Humauaca_%281%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:45._Humauaca_(1).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A view on globalization, titled <i>Somos cultura que camina en un mundo globalizado</i> ("We are a culture walking in a globalized world"). The mural is located in <a href="/wiki/Humahuaca" title="Humahuaca">Humahuaca</a> in the north of <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1982, Mexico announced that it could not meet its foreign debt payment obligations, inaugurating a <a href="/wiki/Latin_American_debt_crisis" title="Latin American debt crisis">debt crisis</a> that would "discredit" Latin American economies throughout the decade.<sup id="cite_ref-Hershberg_&amp;_Rosen_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hershberg_&amp;_Rosen-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> This debt crisis would lead to <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberal</a> reforms that would instigate many social movements in the region. A "reversal of development" reigned over Latin America, seen through negative economic growth, declines in industrial production, and thus, falling living standards for the middle and lower classes.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Governments made financial security their primary policy goal over social security, enacting new neoliberal economic policies that implemented privatization of previously national industries and <a href="/wiki/Informal_sector" class="mw-redirect" title="Informal sector">informalization</a> of labor.<sup id="cite_ref-Hershberg_&amp;_Rosen_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hershberg_&amp;_Rosen-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In an effort to bring more investors to these industries, these governments also embraced <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a> through more open interactions with the international economy.
</p><p>Significantly, as democracy spread across much of Latin America, the realm of government became more inclusive (a trend that proved conducive to social movements), the economic ventures remained exclusive to a few elite groups within society. Neoliberal restructuring consistently redistributed income upward while denying political responsibility to provide social welfare rights, and though <a href="/wiki/Development_studies" title="Development studies">development</a> projects took place throughout the region, both inequality and poverty increased.<sup id="cite_ref-Hershberg_&amp;_Rosen_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hershberg_&amp;_Rosen-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Feeling excluded from these new projects, the lower classes took ownership of their own democracy through a revitalization of social movements in Latin America.
</p><p>Both urban and rural populations had serious grievances as a result of the above economic and global trends and have voiced them in mass demonstrations. Some of the largest and most violent of these have been protests against cuts in urban services, such as the <a href="/wiki/Caracazo" title="Caracazo">Caracazo</a> in Venezuela and the <a href="/wiki/Argentinazo" class="mw-redirect" title="Argentinazo">Argentinazo</a> in Argentina.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnston_&amp;_Almeida_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston_&amp;_Almeida-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MST_International_Socialist46623.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/MST_International_Socialist46623.jpeg/220px-MST_International_Socialist46623.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/MST_International_Socialist46623.jpeg/330px-MST_International_Socialist46623.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/MST_International_Socialist46623.jpeg/440px-MST_International_Socialist46623.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1923" data-file-height="1339" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MST_International_Socialist46623.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Children singing the Internationale, 20th anniversary of MST</div></div></div>
<p>Rural movements have made diverse demands related to unequal land distribution, displacement at the hands of development projects and dams, environmental and indigenous concerns, neoliberal agricultural restructuring, and insufficient means of livelihood. These movements have benefited considerably from transnational support from conservationists and <a href="/wiki/International_nongovernmental_organizations" class="mw-redirect" title="International nongovernmental organizations">INGOs</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Landless_Workers%27_Movement" title="Landless Workers&#39; Movement">Movement of Rural Landless Workers</a> (MST) is perhaps the largest contemporary Latin American social movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnston_&amp;_Almeida_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston_&amp;_Almeida-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> As indigenous populations are primarily rural, indigenous movements account for a large portion of rural social movements, including the <a href="/wiki/Zapatista_Army_of_National_Liberation" title="Zapatista Army of National Liberation">Zapatista rebellion</a> in Mexico, the <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_Indigenous_Nationalities_of_Ecuador" title="Confederation of Indigenous Nationalities of Ecuador">Confederation of Indigenous Nationalities of Ecuador</a> (<a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_Indigenous_Nationalities_of_Ecuador" title="Confederation of Indigenous Nationalities of Ecuador">CONAIE</a>), indigenous organizations in the Amazon region of Ecuador and Bolivia, pan-Mayan communities in Guatemala, and mobilization by the indigenous groups of <a href="/wiki/Yanomami" title="Yanomami">Yanomami</a> peoples in the Amazon, <a href="/wiki/Guna_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Guna people">Kuna</a> peoples in Panama, and Altiplano <a href="/wiki/Aymara_people" title="Aymara people">Aymara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quechua_people" title="Quechua people">Quechua</a> peoples in Bolivia.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnston_&amp;_Almeida_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston_&amp;_Almeida-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Other significant types of social movements include labor struggles and strikes, such as <a href="/wiki/Recovered_factories" class="mw-redirect" title="Recovered factories">recovered factories</a> in Argentina, as well as gender-based movements such as the <a href="/wiki/Mothers_of_the_Plaza_de_Mayo" title="Mothers of the Plaza de Mayo">Mothers of the Plaza de Mayo</a> in Argentina and protests against <a href="/wiki/Maquila" class="mw-redirect" title="Maquila">maquila</a> production, which is largely a women's issue because of how it draws on women for cheap labor.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnston_&amp;_Almeida_96-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston_&amp;_Almeida-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Modern era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2000s_commodities_boom" title="2000s commodities boom">2000s commodities boom</a> caused positive effects for many Latin American economies. Another trend is the rapidly increasing importance of the <a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93Latin_America_relations" title="China–Latin America relations">relations with China</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AFP2011_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AFP2011-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the end of the commodity boom in the 2010s, economic stagnation or recession resulted in some countries. As a result, the left-wing governments of the <a href="/wiki/Pink_Tide" class="mw-redirect" title="Pink Tide">Pink Tide</a> lost support. The worst-hit was Venezuela, which is facing severe <a href="/wiki/Crisis_in_Bolivarian_Venezuela" class="mw-redirect" title="Crisis in Bolivarian Venezuela">social and economic upheaval</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Odebrecht#Legal_problems" title="Odebrecht">corruption scandal of Odebrecht</a>, a Brazilian conglomerate, has raised allegations of corruption across the region's governments (see <a href="/wiki/Operation_Car_Wash" title="Operation Car Wash">Operation Car Wash</a>). The bribery ring has become the largest corruption scandal in Latin American history.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> As of July 2017, the highest ranking politicians charged were former Brazilian President <a href="/wiki/Luiz_In%C3%A1cio_Lula_da_Silva" title="Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva">Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva</a> (arrested)<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> and former Peruvian Presidents <a href="/wiki/Ollanta_Humala" title="Ollanta Humala">Ollanta Humala</a> (arrested) and <a href="/wiki/Alejandro_Toledo" title="Alejandro Toledo">Alejandro Toledo</a> (fugitive, fled to the US).<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
