<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_groups">Ethnic groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Ethnic groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Latin_America" title="Ethnic groups in Latin America">Ethnic groups in Latin America</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Genetic_variation_of_mestizo_populations_in_Latin_America.PNG" class="image"><img alt="Triangle diagrams of genetic makeup of Mexico City and Quetalmahue, Chile" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Genetic_variation_of_mestizo_populations_in_Latin_America.PNG/250px-Genetic_variation_of_mestizo_populations_in_Latin_America.PNG" decoding="async" width="250" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Genetic_variation_of_mestizo_populations_in_Latin_America.PNG/375px-Genetic_variation_of_mestizo_populations_in_Latin_America.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Genetic_variation_of_mestizo_populations_in_Latin_America.PNG/500px-Genetic_variation_of_mestizo_populations_in_Latin_America.PNG 2x" data-file-width="838" data-file-height="341" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Genetic_variation_of_mestizo_populations_in_Latin_America.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Mexican mestizo population is the most diverse in Latin America, with people being either largely European or Amerindian rather than having a uniform admixture. Distribution of Admixture Estimates for Individuals from Mexico City and Quetalmahue (indigenous community in Chile).<sup id="cite_ref-Plos_genetics_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Plos_genetics-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The inhabitants of Latin America are of a variety of ancestries, ethnic groups, and races, making the region one of the most diverse in the world. The specific composition varies from country to country: some have a predominance of European-Amerindian or more commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">Mestizo</a> or <a href="/wiki/Castizo" title="Castizo">Castizo</a> depending on the admixture, population; in others, <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Amerindians</a> are a majority; some are dominated by inhabitants of <a href="/wiki/White_Latin_American" class="mw-redirect" title="White Latin American">European</a> ancestry; and some countries' populations are primarily <a href="/wiki/Mulatto" title="Mulatto">Mulatto</a>. Various <a href="/wiki/Afro-Latin_Americans" title="Afro-Latin Americans">black</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asian_Latin_Americans" title="Asian Latin Americans">Asian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zambo" title="Zambo">Zambo</a> (mixed black and Amerindian) minorities are also identified regularly. People with European ancestry are the largest single group, and along with people of part-European ancestry, they combine to make up approximately 80% of the population,<sup id="cite_ref-CIAEG_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIAEG-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> or even more.<sup id="cite_ref-Lizcano_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lizcano-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Jon Aske:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Before Hispanics became such a 'noticeable' group in the U.S., the distinction between black and white was the major racial division and according to the <a href="/wiki/One-drop_rule" title="One-drop rule">one-drop rule</a> adhered to by the culture at large, one drop of African ancestry usually meant that the person was Black. ...
</p><p>The notion of <b>racial continuum</b> and a separation of race (or skin color) and ethnicity, on the other hand, is the norm in most of Latin America. In the Spanish and Portuguese empires, racial mixing or miscegenation was the norm and something that the Spanish and Portuguese had grown rather accustomed to during the hundreds of years of contact with Arabs and North Africans in the Iberian peninsula. But, demographics may have made this inevitable as well. Thus, for example, of the approximately 13.5 million people who lived in the Spanish colonies in 1800 before independence only about one fifth were white. This contrasts with the U.S., where more than four fifths were whites (out of a population of 5.3 million in 1801, 900,000 were slaves, plus approximately 60,000 free blacks). ...
</p><p>The fact of the recognition of a racial continuum in Hispanic American (sic) does not mean that there wasn't discrimination, which there was, or that there wasn't an obsession with race, or 'castes', as they were sometimes called. ...
</p><p>In areas with large indigenous Amerindian populations, a racial mixture resulted, which is known in Spanish as <i>mestizos</i> ... who are a majority in Mexico, Central America and most of South America. Similarly, when African slaves were brought to the Caribbean region and Brazil, where there was very little indigenous presence left, unions between them and Spanish produced a population of mixed <i>mulatos</i> ... who are a majority of the population in many of those Spanish-speaking Caribbean basin countries (Cuba, Dominican Republic, Puerto Rico, Colombia, and Venezuela).
</p>
</blockquote><p><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Aske has also written that:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Spanish colonization was rather different from later English, or British, colonization of North America. They had different <b>systems of colonization</b> and different methods of subjugation. While the English were primarily interested in grabbing land, the Spanish in addition had a mandate to incorporate the land's inhabitants into their society, something which was achieved by religious conversion and sexual unions which produced a new 'race' of <i>mestizos</i>, a mixture of Europeans and indigenous peoples. <i>mestizos</i> (sic) form the majority of the population in Mexico, Central America, and much of South America. Racial mixing or miscegenation, after all, was something that the Spanish and Portuguese had been accustomed to during the hundreds of years of contact with Arabs and North Africans.
</p><p>Similarly, later on, when African slaves were introduced into the Caribbean basin region, unions between them and Spaniards produced a population of <i>mulatos</i>, who are a majority of the population in the Caribbean islands (the Antilles) (Cuba, Dominican Republic, Puerto Rico), as well as other areas of the Caribbean region (Colombia, Venezuela and parts of the Central American Caribbean coast). <i>mestizos</i> (sic) and <i>mulatos</i> may not have always have been first class citizens in their countries, but they were never disowned in the way the outcomes of unions of Europeans and Native Americans were in the British colonies, where interracial marriages were taboo and one drop of Black or Amerindian blood was enough to make the person 'impure'.
</p>
</blockquote><p><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In his famous 1963 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rise_of_the_West" title="The Rise of the West">The Rise of the West</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/William_Hardy_McNeill" class="mw-redirect" title="William Hardy McNeill">William Hardy McNeill</a> wrote that:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Racially mixed societies arose in most of Spanish and Portuguese America, compounded in varying proportions from European, Indian, and Negro strands. Fairly frequent resort to manumission mitigated the hardships of slavery in those areas; and the Catholic church positively encouraged marriages between white immigrants and Indian women as a remedy for sexual immorality. However, in the southern English colonies and in most of the Caribbean islands, the importation of Negro slaves created a much more sharply polarized biracial society. Strong race feeling and the servile status of nearly all Negroes interdicted intermarriage, practically if not legally. Such discrimination did not prevent interbreeding; but children of mixed parentage were assigned to the status of their mothers. Mulattoes and Indian half-breeds were thereby excluded from the white community. In Spanish (and, with some differences, Portuguese) territories a more elaborate and less oppressive principle of racial discrimination established itself. The handful of persons who had been born in the homelands claimed topmost social prestige; next came those of purely European descent; while beneath ranged the various racial blends to form a social pyramid whose numerous racial distinctions meant that no one barrier could become as ugly and inpenetrable as that dividing whites from Negroes in the English, Dutch, and French colonies.
</p></blockquote><p><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Thomas C. Wright, meanwhile, has written that:
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The demographic makeup of colonial Latin America became more complex when, as the native population declined, the Portuguese, Spanish, and the French in Haiti turned to Africa for labor, as did the British in North America. The tricontinental heritage that characterizes Latin America, then, is shared by the United States, but even a casual examination reveals that the outcome of the complex interaction of different peoples has varied. While miscegenation among the three races certainly occurred in North America, it appears to have been much less common than in Latin America. Furthermore, offspring of such liaisons were not recognized as belonging to new, distinct racial categories in North America as they were in Latin America. The terms mestizo or <i>mameluco</i>, mulatto, the general term <i>castas</i>, and dozens of subcategories of racial identity frankly recognized the outcomes of interracial sexual activity in Latin America and established a continuum of race rather than the unrealistic absolute categories of white, black, or Indian as used in the United States. (The U.S. Census Bureau's forms did not allow individuals to list more than one race until 2000.)
</p></blockquote><p><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map-Romance_Latin_America.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Map-Romance_Latin_America.svg/220px-Map-Romance_Latin_America.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Map-Romance_Latin_America.svg/330px-Map-Romance_Latin_America.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Map-Romance_Latin_America.svg/440px-Map-Romance_Latin_America.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map-Romance_Latin_America.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Linguistic map of Latin America. Spanish in green, Portuguese in orange, and French in blue.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> is the predominant language of Latin America. It is spoken as first language by about 60% of the population. Portuguese is spoken by about 34%, and about 6% speak other languages such as Quechua, <a href="/wiki/Mayan_languages" title="Mayan languages">Mayan languages</a>, Guaraní, Aymara, Nahuatl, English, French, Dutch and Italian. Portuguese is spoken only in Brazil (<a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Portuguese" title="Brazilian Portuguese">Brazilian Portuguese</a>), the biggest and most populous country in the region. Spanish is the official language of most of the rest of the countries and territories on the Latin American mainland (<a href="/wiki/Spanish_language_in_the_Americas" title="Spanish language in the Americas">Spanish language in the Americas</a>), as well as in <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> (where it is co-official with English), and the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>. <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> and in the French <a href="/wiki/Overseas_department" class="mw-redirect" title="Overseas department">overseas departments</a> of <a href="/wiki/Guadeloupe" title="Guadeloupe">Guadeloupe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martinique" title="Martinique">Martinique</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">Guiana</a>, and the French <a href="/wiki/Overseas_collectivity" title="Overseas collectivity">overseas collectivity</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Pierre_and_Miquelon" title="Saint Pierre and Miquelon">Saint Pierre and Miquelon</a>; it is also spoken by some Panamanians of Afro-Antillean descent. <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> is the official language in <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aruba" title="Aruba">Aruba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cura%C3%A7ao" title="Curaçao">Curaçao</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_Antilles" title="Netherlands Antilles">Netherlands Antilles</a>. (As Dutch is a <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic language</a>, these territories are not necessarily considered part of Latin America.) However, the native language of Aruba, <a href="/wiki/Bonaire" title="Bonaire">Bonaire</a>, and Curaçao, is <a href="/wiki/Papiamento" title="Papiamento">Papiamento</a>, a creole language largely based on Portuguese and Spanish and has a considerable influence coming from the Dutch language and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese-based_creole_languages" title="Portuguese-based creole languages">Portuguese-based creole languages</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map-Most_Widely_Spoken_Native_Languages_in_Latin_America.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Map-Most_Widely_Spoken_Native_Languages_in_Latin_America.png/220px-Map-Most_Widely_Spoken_Native_Languages_in_Latin_America.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Map-Most_Widely_Spoken_Native_Languages_in_Latin_America.png/330px-Map-Most_Widely_Spoken_Native_Languages_in_Latin_America.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0c/Map-Most_Widely_Spoken_Native_Languages_in_Latin_America.png 2x" data-file-width="390" data-file-height="450" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map-Most_Widely_Spoken_Native_Languages_in_Latin_America.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b><span style="color:blue">Quechua</span></b>, <b><span style="background-color: black"><span style="color:#f9dc38">Guaraní</span></span></b>, <b><span style="background-color: black"><span style="color:#ff9922">Aymara</span></span></b>, <b><span style="color:#cc0000">Náhuatl</span></b>, <b><span style="color:#006600">Lenguas Mayas</span></b>, <b>Mapudungun</b></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indigenous_languages_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous languages of the Americas">Amerindian languages</a> are widely spoken in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, and to a lesser degree, in <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> amongst other countries. In Latin American countries not named above, the population of speakers of indigenous languages tend to be very small or even non-existent (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a>). Mexico is possibly the only country that contains a wider variety of indigenous languages than any Latin American country, but the most spoken language is Nahuatl.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quechua_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Quechua languages">Quechua</a> is an official language, alongside Spanish and any other indigenous language in the areas where they predominate. In <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, while holding no official status, the closely related <a href="/wiki/Quichua" class="mw-redirect" title="Quichua">Quichua</a> is a recognized language of the indigenous people under the country's constitution; however, it is only spoken by a few groups in the country's highlands. In <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aymara_language" title="Aymara language">Aymara</a>, Quechua and Guaraní hold official status alongside Spanish. <a href="/wiki/Guaran%C3%AD_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Guaraní language">Guaraní</a>, along with Spanish, is an official language of <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a>, and is spoken by a majority of the population (who are, for the most part, bilingual), and it is co-official with Spanish in the <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentine</a> province of <a href="/wiki/Corrientes" title="Corrientes">Corrientes</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, Spanish is the official language, but on the country's Caribbean coast English and indigenous languages such as <a href="/wiki/Miskito_language" title="Miskito language">Miskito</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sumo_languages" title="Sumo languages">Sumo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rama_language" title="Rama language">Rama</a> also hold official status. <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> recognizes all indigenous languages spoken within its territory as official, though fewer than 1% of its population are native speakers of these languages. <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a> is one of the 62 native languages spoken by indigenous people in Mexico, which are officially recognized by the government as "national languages" along with Spanish.
</p><p>Other European languages spoken in Latin America include: <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, by some groups in <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, as well as in nearby countries that may or may not be considered Latin American, like <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a>, and spoken by descendants of British settlers in Argentina &amp; Chile; <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, in southern <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, portions of <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a>; <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>, in Brazil, Argentina, Venezuela, and <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> in southern Brazil and Argentina; and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, in southern Argentina.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Yiddish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Yiddish language">Yiddish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a> are possible to be heard around Buenos Aires and São Paulo especially.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Non-European or Asian languages include <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> in Brazil, Peru, Bolivia, and Paraguay, <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a> in Brazil, Argentina, Paraguay, and Chile, <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> in Argentina, Brazil, Colombia, Venezuela, and Chile, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> throughout South America.
</p><p>In several nations, especially in the Caribbean region, <a href="/wiki/Creole_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Creole languages">creole languages</a> are spoken. The most widely spoken creole language in Latin America and the Caribbean is <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Creole" title="Haitian Creole">Haitian Creole</a>, the predominant language of <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>; it is derived primarily from French and certain West African tongues with <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Amerindian</a>, English, Portuguese and Spanish influences as well. Creole languages of mainland Latin America, similarly, are derived from European languages and various African tongues.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Garifuna_language" title="Garifuna language">Garifuna language</a> is spoken along the Caribbean coast in <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a> mostly by the <a href="/wiki/Garifuna_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Garifuna people">Garifuna people</a> a mixed race <a href="/wiki/Zambo" title="Zambo">Zambo</a> people who were the result of mixing between Indigenous Caribbeans and escaped Black slaves. Primarily an <a href="/wiki/Arawakan_languages" title="Arawakan languages">Arawakan language</a>, it has influences from Caribbean and European languages.
</p><p>Archaeologists have deciphered over 15 pre-Columbian distinct writing systems from mesoamerican societies. the ancient <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya</a> had the most sophisticated textually written language, but since texts were largely confined to the religious and administrative elite, traditions were passed down orally. oral traditions also prevailed in other major indigenous groups including, but not limited to the <a href="/wiki/Aztecs" title="Aztecs">Aztecs</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a> speakers, <a href="/wiki/Quechua_people" title="Quechua people">Quechua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aymara_people" title="Aymara people">Aymara</a> of the Andean regions, the <a href="/wiki/K%27iche%27_people" class="mw-redirect" title="K&#39;iche&#39; people">Quiché</a> of Central America, the <a href="/wiki/Tupi-Guaran%C3%AD" class="mw-redirect" title="Tupi-Guaraní">Tupi-Guaraní</a> in today's Brazil, the <a href="/wiki/Guaran%C3%AD_people" title="Guaraní people">Guaraní</a> in Paraguay and the <a href="/wiki/Mapuche" title="Mapuche">Mapuche</a> in Chile.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Latin_America" title="Religion in Latin America">Religion in Latin America</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Santuario_de_Las_Lajas,_Ipiales,_Colombia,_2015-07-21,_DD_21-23_HDR-Edit.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Santuario_de_Las_Lajas%2C_Ipiales%2C_Colombia%2C_2015-07-21%2C_DD_21-23_HDR-Edit.JPG/220px-Santuario_de_Las_Lajas%2C_Ipiales%2C_Colombia%2C_2015-07-21%2C_DD_21-23_HDR-Edit.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Santuario_de_Las_Lajas%2C_Ipiales%2C_Colombia%2C_2015-07-21%2C_DD_21-23_HDR-Edit.JPG/330px-Santuario_de_Las_Lajas%2C_Ipiales%2C_Colombia%2C_2015-07-21%2C_DD_21-23_HDR-Edit.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Santuario_de_Las_Lajas%2C_Ipiales%2C_Colombia%2C_2015-07-21%2C_DD_21-23_HDR-Edit.JPG/440px-Santuario_de_Las_Lajas%2C_Ipiales%2C_Colombia%2C_2015-07-21%2C_DD_21-23_HDR-Edit.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3958" data-file-height="2758" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Santuario_de_Las_Lajas,_Ipiales,_Colombia,_2015-07-21,_DD_21-23_HDR-Edit.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Las_Lajas_Sanctuary" title="Las Lajas Sanctuary">Las Lajas Sanctuary</a> in the southern <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, Department of <a href="/wiki/Nari%C3%B1o_Department" title="Nariño Department">Nariño</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The vast majority of Latin Americans are Christians (90%),<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> mostly <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholics</a> belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> About 70% of the Latin American population consider themselves Catholic.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the detailed Pew multi-country survey in 2014, 69% of the Latin American population is Catholic and 19% is Protestant. Protestants are 26% in Brazil and over 40% in much of Central America. More than half of these are converts from Roman Catholicism.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Due to economic, social and security developments that are affecting the region in recent decades, the focus is now the change from net immigration to net <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">emigration</a>. About 10 million Mexicans live in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> 31.7 million Americans listed their ancestry as Mexican as of 2010, or roughly 10% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> During the initial stage of the Spanish colonization of the Philippines which were around the 1600s, about 16,500 soldiers levied from <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> were sent together with 600 Spanish officers to fight wars, settle, colonize and build cities and <a href="/wiki/Presidios" class="mw-redirect" title="Presidios">Presidios</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> These 16,500 Peruvians and Mexicans supplemented the Native Malay Population which then reached 667,612 people.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> This initial group of Latin American soldier-settler founders had spread their genes among the sparesly populated Philippines.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> This resulted into a spread of Latin American admixture among Filipinos as evidenced by a large number of Filipinos possessing Native American ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Y-DNA" class="mw-redirect" title="Y-DNA">Y-DNA</a> compilation organized by the Genetic Company "Applied Biosystems" found that 13.33% of the Filipino Male Population sampled from across the country had Y-DNA of Latin American and Spanish origins.<sup id="cite_ref-Applied_Biosystems_Genetic_Study_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Applied_Biosystems_Genetic_Study-127">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, according to a survey dated from 1870 conducted by German ethnologist <a href="/wiki/Fedor_Jagor" title="Fedor Jagor">Fedor Jagor</a> of the population of <a href="/wiki/Luzon" title="Luzon">Luzon</a> island (Which holds half the citizens of the Philippines) 1/3rd of the people possess varying degrees of Spanish and Latin American ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> According to the 2005 Colombian census or DANE, about 3,331,107 Colombians currently live abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-pstalker_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pstalker-129">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The number of Brazilians living overseas is estimated at about 2 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> An estimated 1.5 to two million Salvadorans reside in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> At least 1.5 million Ecuadorians have gone abroad, mainly to the United States and Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Approximately 1.5 million Dominicans live abroad, mostly in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> More than 1.3 million Cubans live abroad, most of them in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> It is estimated that over 800,000 Chileans live abroad, mainly in Argentina, the United States, Canada, Australia and Sweden.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> An estimated 700,000 Bolivians were living in Argentina as of 2006 and another 33,000 in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brazilians_in_Japan" title="Brazilians in Japan">Japanese Brazilian immigrants to Japan</a> numbered 250,000 in 2004, constituting Japan's second-largest immigrant population. Their experiences bear similarities to those of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Peruvian" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese Peruvian">Japanese Peruvian</a> immigrants, who are often relegated to low income jobs typically occupied by foreigners.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Central Americans living abroad in 2005 were 3,314,300,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> of which 1,128,701 were <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">Salvadorans</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> 685,713 were <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemalans</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> 683,520 were <a href="/wiki/Nicaraguans" title="Nicaraguans">Nicaraguans</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> 414,955 were <a href="/wiki/Hondurans" title="Hondurans">Hondurans</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> 215,240 were <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panamanians</a><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> and 127,061 were <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Ricans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For the period 2000–2005, Chile, Costa Rica, Panama, and Venezuela were the only countries with global positive migration rates, in terms of their yearly averages.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of the <a href="/wiki/2010_Haiti_Earthquake" class="mw-redirect" title="2010 Haiti Earthquake">2010 Haiti Earthquake</a> and its social and economic impact, there was a significant migration of Haitians to other Latin American countries. During the presidency of <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez" title="Hugo Chávez">Hugo Chávez</a> and his successor <a href="/wiki/Nicol%C3%A1s_Maduro" title="Nicolás Maduro">Nicolás Maduro</a>, over 3.2 million people fled <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Venezuelan_refugee_crisis" title="Venezuelan refugee crisis">Venezuelan refugee crisis</a> as <a href="/wiki/Crisis_in_Bolivarian_Venezuela" class="mw-redirect" title="Crisis in Bolivarian Venezuela">socioeconomic conditions and the quality of life worsened</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EUdiaspora_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUdiaspora-146">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NEWSWEEKdiaspora_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEWSWEEKdiaspora-147">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EUpercent10_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EUpercent10-148">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The countries of Latin America seek to strengthen links between migrants and their states of origin, while promoting their integration in the receiving state. These Emigrant Policies focus on the rights, obligations and opportunities for participation of emigrated citizens who already live outside the borders of the country of origin. Research on Latin America shows that the extension of policies towards migrants is linked to a focus on civil rights and state benefits that can positively influence integration in recipient countries. In addition, the tolerance of dual citizenship has spread more in Latin America than in any other region of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Latin_America" title="Education in Latin America">Education in Latin America</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_map_of_countries_by_literacy_rate.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/World_map_of_countries_by_literacy_rate.svg/220px-World_map_of_countries_by_literacy_rate.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="97" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/World_map_of_countries_by_literacy_rate.svg/330px-World_map_of_countries_by_literacy_rate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/World_map_of_countries_by_literacy_rate.svg/440px-World_map_of_countries_by_literacy_rate.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="415" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_map_of_countries_by_literacy_rate.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World map indicating literacy rate by country in 2015 (2015 CIA <i><a href="/wiki/World_Factbook" class="mw-redirect" title="World Factbook">World Factbook</a></i>). Grey = no data.</div></div></div>
<p>Despite significant progress, education access and school completion remains unequal in Latin America. The region has made great progress in educational coverage; almost all children attend primary school and access to secondary education has increased considerably. Quality issues such as poor teaching methods, lack of appropriate equipment and overcrowding exist throughout the region. These issues lead to adolescents dropping out of the educational system early.<sup id="cite_ref-Welti_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welti-150">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Most educational systems in the region have implemented various types of administrative and institutional reforms that have enabled reach for places and communities that had no access to education services in the early 1990s. Compared to prior generations, Latin American youth have seen an increase in their levels of education. On average, they have completed two years schooling more than their parents.<sup id="cite_ref-Welti_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welti-150">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, there are still 23 million children in the region between the ages of 4 and 17 outside of the formal education system. Estimates indicate that 30% of preschool age children (ages 4–5) do not attend school, and for the most vulnerable populations, the poor and rural, this calculation exceeds 40 percent. Among primary school age children (ages 6 to 12), coverage is almost universal; however there is still a need to incorporate 5 million children in the primary education system. These children live mostly in remote areas, are indigenous or Afro-descendants and live in extreme poverty.<sup id="cite_ref-Survey_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Survey-151">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Among people between the ages of 13 and 17 years, only 80% are full-time students in the education system; among them only 66% advance to secondary school. These percentages are lower among vulnerable population groups: only 75% of the poorest youth between the ages of 13 and 17 years attend school. Tertiary education has the lowest coverage, with only 70% of people between the ages of 18 and 25 years outside of the education system. Currently, more than half of low income children or living in rural areas fail to complete nine years of education.<sup id="cite_ref-Survey_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Survey-151">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crime_and_violence">Crime and violence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Crime and violence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Crime_and_violence_in_Latin_America" title="Crime and violence in Latin America">Crime and violence in Latin America</a></div>
<p>Latin America and the Caribbean have been cited by numerous sources to be the most dangerous regions in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Studies have shown that Latin America contains the majority of the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_by_murder_rate" title="List of cities by murder rate">most dangerous cities</a>. Many analysts attribute the reason to why the region has such an alarming crime rate and criminal culture is largely due to <a href="/wiki/Social_inequality" title="Social inequality">social</a> and <a href="/wiki/Income_inequality" class="mw-redirect" title="Income inequality">income inequality</a> within the region, they say that growing social inequality is fueling crime in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Many agree that the prison crisis will not be resolved until the gap between the rich and the poor is addressed.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Homicide_rates_per_100000_people_world,_UNODC_2012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Homicide_rates_per_100000_people_world%2C_UNODC_2012.jpg/250px-Homicide_rates_per_100000_people_world%2C_UNODC_2012.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Homicide_rates_per_100000_people_world%2C_UNODC_2012.jpg/375px-Homicide_rates_per_100000_people_world%2C_UNODC_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Homicide_rates_per_100000_people_world%2C_UNODC_2012.jpg/500px-Homicide_rates_per_100000_people_world%2C_UNODC_2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="820" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Homicide_rates_per_100000_people_world,_UNODC_2012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2012 map of countries by <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_intentional_homicide_rate" title="List of countries by intentional homicide rate">homicide rate</a>. As of 2015, the Latin American countries with the highest rates were El Salvador (108.64 per 100,000 people), Honduras (63.75) and Venezuela (57.15). The countries with the lowest rates were Chile (3.59), Cuba (4.72) and Argentina (6.53).</div></div></div>
<p>Crime and violence prevention and public security are now important issues for governments and citizens in Latin America and the Caribbean region. Homicide rates in Latin America are the highest in the world. From the early 1980s through the mid-1990s, homicide rates increased by 50 percent. Latin America and the Caribbean experienced more than 2.5 million <a href="/wiki/Murder" title="Murder">murders</a> between 2000 and 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> There were a total of 63,880 murders in Brazil in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The major victims of such homicides are young men, 69 percent of whom are between the ages of 15 and 19 years old. Countries with the highest homicide rate per year per 100,000 inhabitants as of 2015 were: <a href="/wiki/El_Salvador" title="El Salvador">El Salvador</a> 109, <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a> 64, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> 57, <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a> 43, <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a> 34.4, <a href="/wiki/St._Kitts_and_Nevis" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Kitts and Nevis">St. Kitts and Nevis</a> 34, <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">Guatemala</a> 34, <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a> 31, <a href="/wiki/The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas">the Bahamas</a> 30, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> 26.7, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> 26.5, the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> 22, <a href="/wiki/St._Lucia" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Lucia">St. Lucia</a> 22, <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a> 19, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> 16, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a> 16, <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a> 13, <a href="/wiki/Grenada" title="Grenada">Grenada</a> 13, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a> 12, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a> 12, <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> 12, <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a> 11, <a href="/wiki/Antigua_and_Barbuda" title="Antigua and Barbuda">Antigua and Barbuda</a> 11, and <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> 10.<sup id="cite_ref-Homicide_Statistics_database._157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Homicide_Statistics_database.-157">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Most of the top countries with the highest homicide rates are in Africa and Latin America. Countries in Central America, like El Salvador and Honduras, top the list of homicides in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-murder_rates2_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-murder_rates2-158">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Brazil has more overall homicides than any country in the world, at 50,108, accounting for one in 10 globally. Crime-related violence in Latin America represents the most threat to public health, striking more victims than <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a> or other infectious diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Countries with lowest homicide rate per year per 100,000 inhabitants as of 2015 were: <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> 3, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> 7, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> 7, <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a> 8 and <a href="/wiki/Paraguay" title="Paraguay">Paraguay</a> 9.<sup id="cite_ref-Homicide_Statistics_database._157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Homicide_Statistics_database.-157">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_2">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latin_American_economy" title="Latin American economy">Latin American economy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sampa_Noite.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Sampa_Noite.jpg/200px-Sampa_Noite.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Sampa_Noite.jpg/300px-Sampa_Noite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Sampa_Noite.jpg/400px-Sampa_Noite.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4819" data-file-height="3213" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sampa_Noite.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Brazil.svg/22px-Flag_of_Brazil.svg.png" decoding="async" width="22" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Brazil.svg/33px-Flag_of_Brazil.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Brazil.svg/43px-Flag_of_Brazil.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="504" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Size">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Goldman_Sachs" title="Goldman Sachs">Goldman Sachs</a>' <a href="/wiki/BRICS" title="BRICS">BRICS</a> review of emerging economies, by 2050 the largest economies in the world will be as follows: China, United States, India, Japan, Germany, United Kingdom, Brazil and Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.-_Paseo.Reforma.Skyline_CDMX_2016_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.-_Paseo.Reforma.Skyline_CDMX_2016_2.jpg/200px-Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.-_Paseo.Reforma.Skyline_CDMX_2016_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="93" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.-_Paseo.Reforma.Skyline_CDMX_2016_2.jpg/300px-Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.-_Paseo.Reforma.Skyline_CDMX_2016_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.-_Paseo.Reforma.Skyline_CDMX_2016_2.jpg/400px-Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.-_Paseo.Reforma.Skyline_CDMX_2016_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2719" data-file-height="1267" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ciudad.de.Mexico.City.-_Paseo.Reforma.Skyline_CDMX_2016_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Flag_of_Mexico.svg/23px-Flag_of_Mexico.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="13" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Flag_of_Mexico.svg/35px-Flag_of_Mexico.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Flag_of_Mexico.svg/46px-Flag_of_Mexico.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="980" data-file-height="560" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a></div></div></div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Costanera_Center_Santiago_De_Chile_(192559007).jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Costanera_Center_Santiago_De_Chile_%28192559007%29.jpeg/200px-Costanera_Center_Santiago_De_Chile_%28192559007%29.jpeg" decoding="async" width="200" height="105" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Costanera_Center_Santiago_De_Chile_%28192559007%29.jpeg/300px-Costanera_Center_Santiago_De_Chile_%28192559007%29.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Costanera_Center_Santiago_De_Chile_%28192559007%29.jpeg/400px-Costanera_Center_Santiago_De_Chile_%28192559007%29.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1077" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Costanera_Center_Santiago_De_Chile_(192559007).jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Flag_of_Chile.svg/23px-Flag_of_Chile.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Flag_of_Chile.svg/35px-Flag_of_Chile.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Flag_of_Chile.svg/45px-Flag_of_Chile.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1000" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santiago" title="Santiago">Santiago</a></div></div></div>

</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Over the past two centuries, Latin America’s GDP per capita has fluctuated around world average. However, there is a substantial gap between Latin America and the developed economies. In the Andean region this gap can be a consequence of low human capital among Inca Indios in Pre-Columbian times. It is evident that the numeracy value of Peruvian Indios in the early 16th century was just half of the numeracy of the Spanish and Portuguese.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Between 1820 and 2008, this gap widened from 0.8 to 2.7 times.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Since 1980, Latin America also lost growth versus the world average. Many nations such as those in Asia have joined others on a rapid economic growth path, but Latin America has grown at slower pace and its share of world output declined from 9.5% in 1980 to 7.8% in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_of_living">Standard of living</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Standard of living">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Latin America is the region with the highest levels of income inequality in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> The following table lists all the countries in Latin America indicating a valuation of the country's <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>, <a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a> at purchasing power parity per capita, measurement of inequality through the <a href="/wiki/Gini_index" class="mw-redirect" title="Gini index">Gini index</a>, measurement of poverty through the <a href="/wiki/Human_Poverty_Index" title="Human Poverty Index">Human Poverty Index</a>, measurement of extreme poverty based on people living under 1.25 dollars a day, <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a>, murder rates and a measurement of safety through the <a href="/wiki/Global_Peace_Index" title="Global Peace Index">Global Peace Index</a>. Green cells indicate the best performance in each category while red indicates the lowest.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Inequality">Inequality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Inequality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wealth_inequality_in_Latin_America" title="Wealth inequality in Latin America">Wealth inequality in Latin America</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wealth_inequality" class="mw-redirect" title="Wealth inequality">Wealth inequality</a> in Latin America and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> remains a serious issue despite strong economic growth and improved social indicators over the past decade. A report released in 2013 by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Department_of_Economic_and_Social_Affairs" title="United Nations Department of Economic and Social Affairs">UN Department of Economic and Social Affairs</a> entitled <i>Inequality Matters. Report of the World Social Situation</i>, observed that: ‘Declines in the wage share have been attributed to the impact of labour-saving technological change and to a general weakening of labour market regulations and institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-178">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Such declines are likely to affect individuals in the middle and bottom of the income distribution disproportionately, since they rely mostly on labour income.’ In addition, the report noted that ‘highly-unequal land distribution has created social and political tensions and is a source of economic inefficiency, as small landholders frequently lack access to credit and other resources to increase productivity, while big owners may not have had enough incentive to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-178">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_blocs">Trade blocs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Trade blocs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG/220px-New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="460" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG/330px-New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG/440px-New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="1004" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Native <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> crops <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbian Exchange">exchanged globally</a>: maize, tomato, potato, <a href="/wiki/Vanilla" title="Vanilla">vanilla</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rubber" class="mw-redirect" title="Rubber">rubber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cacao_bean" class="mw-redirect" title="Cacao bean">cacao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">tobacco</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Banco_del_Sur.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Banco_del_Sur.jpg/220px-Banco_del_Sur.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Banco_del_Sur.jpg/330px-Banco_del_Sur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Banco_del_Sur.jpg/440px-Banco_del_Sur.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1442" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Banco_del_Sur.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Rafael_Correa" title="Rafael Correa">Rafael Correa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evo_Morales" title="Evo Morales">Evo Morales</a>, <a href="/wiki/N%C3%A9stor_Kirchner" title="Néstor Kirchner">Néstor Kirchner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cristina_Fern%C3%A1ndez" class="mw-redirect" title="Cristina Fernández">Cristina Fernández</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luiz_In%C3%A1cio_Lula_da_Silva" title="Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva">Luiz Inácio Lula da Silva</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicanor_Duarte" title="Nicanor Duarte">Nicanor Duarte</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez" title="Hugo Chávez">Hugo Chávez</a> at the signing of the founding charter of the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_the_South" title="Bank of the South">Bank of the South</a></div></div></div>
<p>The major trade blocs (or agreements) in the region are the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Alliance" title="Pacific Alliance">Pacific Alliance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mercosur" title="Mercosur">Mercosur</a>. Minor blocs or trade agreements are the <a href="/wiki/G3_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="G3 Free Trade Agreement">G3 Free Trade Agreement</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic_%E2%80%93_Central_America_Free_Trade_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Dominican Republic – Central America Free Trade Agreement">Dominican Republic – Central America Free Trade Agreement</a> (DR-CAFTA), the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Community" title="Caribbean Community">Caribbean Community</a> (CARICOM) and the <a href="/wiki/Andean_Community_of_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Andean Community of Nations">Andean Community of Nations</a> (CAN). However, major reconfigurations are taking place along opposing approaches to integration and trade; Venezuela has officially withdrawn from both the CAN and G3 and it has been formally admitted into the Mercosur (pending ratification from the Paraguayan legislature). The president-elect of Ecuador has manifested his intentions of following the same path. This bloc nominally opposes any Free Trade Agreement (FTA) with the United States, although Uruguay has manifested its intention otherwise. <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> are the only four Latin American nations that have an FTA with the United States and Canada, both members of the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">North American Free Trade Agreement</a> (NAFTA).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Imagebysafa2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Imagebysafa2.jpg/220px-Imagebysafa2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Imagebysafa2.jpg/330px-Imagebysafa2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Imagebysafa2.jpg/440px-Imagebysafa2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1248" data-file-height="832" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Imagebysafa2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of <a href="/wiki/Canc%C3%BAn" title="Cancún">Cancún</a>. Mexico is the most visited country in Latin America and 6th in the world.</div></div></div>
<p>Income from tourism is key to the economy of several Latin American countries.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> is the only Latin American country to be ranked in the top 10 worldwide in the number of tourist visits. It received by far the largest number of international tourists, with 39.3 million visitors in 2017, followed by <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, with 6.7 million; the <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, with 6.6 million; <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, with 6.5 million; Dominican Republic, with 6.2 million; <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> with 4.3 million; <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> with 4.0 million. The <a href="/wiki/World_Tourism_Organization" title="World Tourism Organization">World Tourism Organization</a> reports the following destinations as the top six tourism earners for the year 2017: Mexico, with US$21,333 million; the Dominican Republic, with US$7,178 million; Brazil, with US$6,024 million; <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, with US$4,773 million; <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, with US$4,687 million; and <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, with US$4,258 million.<sup id="cite_ref-WTO_Tourism_Highlights_2018_Edition_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WTO_Tourism_Highlights_2018_Edition-181">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Places such as <a href="/wiki/Canc%C3%BAn" title="Cancún">Cancún</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riviera_Maya" title="Riviera Maya">Riviera Maya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gal%C3%A1pagos_Islands" title="Galápagos Islands">Galápagos Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punta_Cana" title="Punta Cana">Punta Cana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chichen_Itza" title="Chichen Itza">Chichen Itza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cartagena,_Colombia" title="Cartagena, Colombia">Cartagena de Indias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cabo_San_Lucas" title="Cabo San Lucas">Cabo San Lucas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Machu_Picchu" title="Machu Picchu">Machu Picchu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Margarita_Island" title="Margarita Island">Margarita Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acapulco" title="Acapulco">Acapulco</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Ignacio_Min%C3%AD" title="San Ignacio Miní">San Ignacio Miní</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">Santo Domingo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salar_de_Uyuni" title="Salar de Uyuni">Salar de Uyuni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punta_del_Este" title="Punta del Este">Punta del Este</a>, <a href="/wiki/Labadee" title="Labadee">Labadee</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Juan,_Puerto_Rico" title="San Juan, Puerto Rico">San Juan</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">La Habana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panama_City" title="Panama City">Panama City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iguaz%C3%BA_Falls" class="mw-redirect" title="Iguazú Falls">Iguazú Falls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Vallarta" title="Puerto Vallarta">Puerto Vallarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Po%C3%A1s_Volcano_National_Park" title="Poás Volcano National Park">Poás Volcano National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vi%C3%B1a_del_Mar" title="Viña del Mar">Viña del Mar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guanajuato_City" title="Guanajuato City">Guanajuato City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bogot%C3%A1" title="Bogotá">Bogotá</a>, <a href="/wiki/Santa_Marta" title="Santa Marta">Santa Marta</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Andr%C3%A9s_(island)" title="San Andrés (island)">San Andrés</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Miguel_de_Allende" title="San Miguel de Allende">San Miguel de Allende</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lima" title="Lima">Lima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guadalajara" title="Guadalajara">Guadalajara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuzco" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuzco">Cuzco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ponce,_Puerto_Rico" title="Ponce, Puerto Rico">Ponce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perito_Moreno_Glacier" title="Perito Moreno Glacier">Perito Moreno Glacier</a> are popular among international visitors in the region.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
